	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Amnesty International</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">London-based international human rights organization</div>

rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.amnesty.org/en">amnesty.org</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Amnesty International</b> (commonly known as <b>Amnesty</b> or <b>AI</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organization</a> with its headquarters in the United Kingdom focused on <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>. The organization claims it has more than eight million members and supporters around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-whoai_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoai-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The stated mission of the organization is to campaign for "a world in which every person enjoys all of the human rights enshrined in the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> and other international human rights instruments."<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amnesty International was founded in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> in 1961, following the publication of the article "<a href="/wiki/The_Forgotten_Prisoners" title="The Forgotten Prisoners">The Forgotten Prisoners</a>" in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> on 28 May 1961,<sup id="cite_ref-Forgotten_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forgotten-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> by the lawyers <a href="/wiki/Peter_Benenson" title="Peter Benenson">Peter Benenson</a> and Philip James. Amnesty draws attention to human rights abuses and campaigns for compliance with <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international laws</a> and standards. It works to mobilize <a href="/wiki/Public_opinion" title="Public opinion">public opinion</a> to generate pressure on governments where abuse takes place.<sup id="cite_ref-aboutai_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aboutai-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Amnesty considers <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">capital punishment</a> to be "the ultimate, irreversible denial of human rights."<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The organization was awarded the 1977 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> for its "defence of human dignity against <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a>,"<sup id="cite_ref-AINobelLec_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AINobelLec-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Prize_in_the_Field_of_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Prize in the Field of Human Rights">United Nations Prize in the Field of Human Rights</a> in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-AIUNAward_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIUNAward-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the field of international <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_rights_organisations" title="List of human rights organisations">human rights organizations</a>, Amnesty has the third longest history, after the <a href="/wiki/International_Federation_for_Human_Rights" title="International Federation for Human Rights">International Federation for Human Rights</a><sup id="cite_ref-ISQ05_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISQ05-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>, and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Slavery_International" title="Anti-Slavery International">Anti-Slavery Society</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1960s">1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Benenson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/bc/Peter_Benenson.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="280" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="220" data-file-height="280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peter_Benenson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Peter_Benenson" title="Peter Benenson">Peter Benenson</a>, the founder of Amnesty International. He worked for Britain's <a href="/wiki/Government_Communications_Headquarters" class="mw-redirect" title="Government Communications Headquarters">GC&amp;CS</a> at <a href="/wiki/Bletchley_Park" title="Bletchley Park">Bletchley Park</a> during World War II.</div></div></div><p>
Amnesty International was founded in London in July 1961 by English labour lawyer <a href="/wiki/Peter_Benenson" title="Peter Benenson">Peter Benenson</a> along with Professor of Law and friend Philip James.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> According to Benenson's own account, he was travelling on the <a href="/wiki/London_Underground" title="London Underground">London Underground</a> on 19 November 1960 when he read that two Portuguese students from <a href="/wiki/Coimbra" title="Coimbra">Coimbra</a> had been sentenced to seven years of imprisonment in Portugal for allegedly "having drunk a toast to liberty".<sup id="cite_ref-fn1_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fn1-11">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Keane_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keane-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Researchers have never traced the alleged newspaper article in question.<sup id="cite_ref-fn1_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fn1-11">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> In 1960, Portugal was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)">Estado Novo</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_de_Oliveira_Salazar" title="António de Oliveira Salazar">António de Oliveira Salazar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The government was authoritarian in nature and strongly <a href="/wiki/Anti-communist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-communist">anti-communist</a>, suppressing enemies of the state as anti-Portuguese. In his significant newspaper article "The Forgotten Prisoners", Benenson later described his reaction as follows: </p><blockquote><p>Open your newspaper any day of the week and you will find a story from somewhere of someone being imprisoned, tortured or executed because his opinions or religion are unacceptable to his government... The newspaper reader feels a sickening sense of impotence. Yet if these feelings of disgust could be united into common action, something effective could be done.<sup id="cite_ref-Forgotten_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forgotten-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Benenson worked with friend <a href="/wiki/Eric_Baker_(activist)" title="Eric Baker (activist)">Eric Baker</a>. Baker was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Religious Society of Friends</a> who had been involved in funding the British <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a> as well as becoming  head of <a href="/wiki/Quaker_Peace_and_Social_Witness" title="Quaker Peace and Social Witness">Quaker Peace and Social Witness</a>, and in his memoirs Benenson described him as "a partner in the launching of the project".<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In consultation with other writers, academics and lawyers and, in particular, Alec Digges, they wrote via <a href="/wiki/Louis_Blom-Cooper" title="Louis Blom-Cooper">Louis Blom-Cooper</a> to <a href="/wiki/David_Astor" title="David Astor">David Astor</a>, editor of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i> newspaper, who, on 28 May 1961, published Benenson's article "The Forgotten Prisoners".  The article brought the reader's attention to those "imprisoned, tortured or executed because his opinions or religion are unacceptable to his government"<sup id="cite_ref-Forgotten_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forgotten-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> or, put another way, to violations, by governments, of articles 18 and 19 of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> (UDHR). The article described these violations occurring, on a global scale, in the context of restrictions to press freedom, to political oppositions, to timely <a href="/wiki/Public_trial" title="Public trial">public trial</a> before impartial courts, and to asylum. It marked the launch of "Appeal for Amnesty, 1961", the aim of which was to mobilize public opinion, quickly and widely, in defence of these individuals, whom Benenson named "Prisoners of Conscience". The "Appeal for Amnesty" was reprinted by a large number of international newspapers. In the same year, Benenson had a book published, <i>Persecution 1961</i>, which detailed the cases of nine <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_conscience" title="Prisoner of conscience">prisoners of conscience</a> investigated and compiled by Benenson and Baker (Maurice Audin, <a href="/wiki/Ashton_Jones" title="Ashton Jones">Ashton Jones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agostinho_Neto" title="Agostinho Neto">Agostinho Neto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Duncan_(anti-apartheid_activist)" title="Patrick Duncan (anti-apartheid activist)">Patrick Duncan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olga_Ivinskaya" title="Olga Ivinskaya">Olga Ivinskaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Taruc" title="Luis Taruc">Luis Taruc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Constantin_Noica" title="Constantin Noica">Constantin Noica</a>, Antonio Amat and <a href="/wiki/Hu_Feng" title="Hu Feng">Hu Feng</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>  In July 1961, the leadership had decided that the appeal would form the basis of a permanent organization, Amnesty, with the first meeting taking place in London. Benenson ensured that all three major political parties were represented, enlisting members of parliament from the <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">the Conservative Party</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">the Liberal Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> On 30 September 1962, it was officially named "Amnesty International". Between the "Appeal for Amnesty, 1961" and September 1962 the organization had been known simply as "Amnesty".<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>What started as a short appeal soon became a permanent international movement working to protect those imprisoned for non-violent expression of their views and to secure worldwide recognition of Articles 18 and 19 of the UDHR. From the very beginning, research and campaigning were present in Amnesty International's work. A library was established for information about prisoners of conscience and a network of local groups, called "THREES" groups, was started.  Each group worked on behalf of three prisoners, one from each of the then three main ideological regions of the world: <a href="/wiki/Communist_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Communist world">communist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capitalist_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitalist world">capitalist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Developing_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing world">developing</a>.
</p><p>By the mid-1960s, Amnesty International's global presence was growing and an International Secretariat and International Executive Committee were established to manage Amnesty International's national organizations, called "Sections", which had appeared in several countries. The international movement was starting to agree on its core principles and techniques. For example, the issue of whether or not to adopt prisoners who had advocated violence, like <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> brought unanimous agreement that it could not give the name of "Prisoner of Conscience" to such prisoners. Aside from the work of the library and groups, Amnesty International's activities were expanding to helping prisoners' families, sending observers to trials, making representations to governments, and finding asylum or overseas employment for prisoners. Its activity and influence were also increasing within intergovernmental organizations; it would be awarded consultative status by the United Nations, the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> before the decade ended.
</p><p>In 1967, Peter Benenson resigned after an independent inquiry did not support his claims that AI had been infiltrated by British agents.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Later he claimed that the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> (CIA) had become involved in Amnesty.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s">1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 1970s, <a href="/wiki/Se%C3%A1n_MacBride" title="Seán MacBride">Seán MacBride</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martin_Ennals" title="Martin Ennals">Martin Ennals</a> led Amnesty International. While continuing to work for prisoners of conscience, Amnesty International's purview widened to include "<a href="/wiki/Fair_trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Fair trial">fair trial</a>" and opposition to long <a href="/wiki/Detention_without_trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Detention without trial">detention without trial</a> (UDHR Article 9), and especially to the torture of prisoners (UDHR Article 5). Amnesty International believed that the reasons underlying torture of prisoners by governments, were either to acquire and obtain information or to quell opposition by the use of terror, or both. Also of concern was the export of more sophisticated torture methods, equipment and teaching by the superpowers to "client states", for example by the United States through <a href="/wiki/CIA_transnational_human_rights_actions" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA transnational human rights actions">some activities of the CIA</a>.
</p><p>Amnesty International drew together reports from countries where torture allegations seemed most persistent and organized an international conference on torture. It sought to influence public opinion to put pressure on national governments by organizing a campaign for the "Abolition of Torture", which ran for several years.
</p><p>Amnesty International's membership increased from 15,000 in 1969<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> to 200,000 by 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> This growth in resources enabled an expansion of its program, "outside of the prison walls", to include work on <a href="/wiki/Forced_disappearance" title="Forced disappearance">"disappearances"</a>, the death penalty and the rights of refugees. A new technique, the "Urgent Action", aimed at mobilizing the membership into action rapidly was pioneered. The first was issued on 19 March 1973, on behalf of Luiz Basilio Rossi, a Brazilian academic, arrested for political reasons.
</p><p>At the intergovernmental level Amnesty International pressed for application of the UN's <a href="/wiki/Standard_Minimum_Rules_for_the_Treatment_of_Prisoners" title="Standard Minimum Rules for the Treatment of Prisoners">Standard Minimum Rules for the Treatment of Prisoners</a> and of existing humanitarian conventions; to secure ratifications of the two <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">UN Covenants on Human Rights</a> in 1976; and was instrumental in obtaining additional instruments and provisions forbidding the practice of maltreatment. Consultative status was granted at the <a href="/wiki/Inter-American_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="Inter-American Commission on Human Rights">Inter-American Commission on Human Rights</a> in 1972.
</p><p>In 1976, Amnesty's British Section started a series of fund-raising events that came to be known as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_Policeman%27s_Balls" class="mw-redirect" title="The Secret Policeman&#39;s Balls">The Secret Policeman's Balls</a></i> series. They were staged in London initially as comedy galas featuring what the <i>Daily Telegraph</i> called "the crème de la crème of the British comedy world"<sup id="cite_ref-Monahan_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monahan-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> including members of comedy troupe <a href="/wiki/Monty_Python" title="Monty Python">Monty Python</a>, and later expanded to also include performances by leading rock musicians. The series was created and developed by <a href="/wiki/Monty_Python" title="Monty Python">Monty Python</a> alumnus <a href="/wiki/John_Cleese" title="John Cleese">John Cleese</a> and entertainment industry executive <a href="/wiki/Martin_Lewis_(humorist)" title="Martin Lewis (humorist)">Martin Lewis</a> working closely with Amnesty staff members <a href="/wiki/Peter_Luff_(Campaigner)" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Luff (Campaigner)">Peter Luff</a> (Assistant Director of Amnesty 1974–78) and subsequently with Peter Walker (Amnesty Fund-Raising Officer 1978–82). Cleese, Lewis and Luff worked together on the first two shows (1976 and 1977). Cleese, Lewis and Walker worked together on the 1979 and 1981 shows, the first to carry what the <i>Daily Telegraph</i> described as the "rather brilliantly re-christened" <i>Secret Policeman's Ball</i> title.<sup id="cite_ref-Monahan_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monahan-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The organization was awarded the 1977 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> for its "defence of human dignity against <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-AINobelLec_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AINobelLec-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Prize_in_the_Field_of_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Prize in the Field of Human Rights">United Nations Prize in the Field of Human Rights</a> in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-AIUNAward_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIUNAward-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1980s">1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1980, Amnesty International was drawing more criticism from governments. The <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> alleged that Amnesty International conducted espionage, the <a href="/wiki/Moroccan_government" class="mw-redirect" title="Moroccan government">Moroccan government</a> denounced it as a defender of lawbreakers, and the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Argentina" title="Government of Argentina">Argentinian government</a> banned Amnesty International's 1983 annual report.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout the 1980s, Amnesty International continued to campaign against torture, and on behalf of prisoners of conscience. New issues emerged, including <a href="/wiki/Extrajudicial_killing" title="Extrajudicial killing">extrajudicial killings</a>, military, security and police transfers, political killings, and disappearances.
</p><p>Towards the end of the decade, the growing number of refugees worldwide became a focus for Amnesty International. While many of the world's refugees of the time had been displaced by war and <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, in adherence to its mandate, Amnesty International concentrated on those forced to flee because of the human rights violations it was seeking to prevent. It argued that rather than focusing on new restrictions on entry for asylum-seekers, governments were to address the human rights violations which were forcing people into exile.
</p><p>Apart from a second campaign on torture during the first half of the decade, two major musical events took place to increase awareness of Amnesty and of human rights (particularly among younger generations) during the mid- to late-1980s. The 1986 <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_of_Hope" class="mw-redirect" title="Conspiracy of Hope">Conspiracy of Hope</a> tour, which played five concerts in the US, and culminated in a daylong show, featuring some thirty-odd acts at Giants Stadium, and the 1988 <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Now!" title="Human Rights Now!">Human Rights Now!</a> world tour. Human Rights Now!, which was timed to coincide with the 40th anniversary of the United Nations' <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> (UDHR), played a series of concerts on five continents over six weeks. Both tours featured some of the most famous musicians and bands of the day.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990s">1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Throughout the 1990s, Amnesty continued to grow, to a membership of over seven million in over 150 countries and territories,<sup id="cite_ref-whoai_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whoai-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> led by Senegalese Secretary General <a href="/wiki/Pierre_San%C3%A9" title="Pierre Sané">Pierre Sané</a>. Amnesty continued to work on a wide range of issues and world events. For example, South African groups joined in 1992 and hosted a visit by Pierre Sané to meet with the <a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">apartheid</a> government to press for an investigation into allegations of police abuse, an end to arms sales to the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> region and the abolition of the death penalty. In particular, Amnesty International brought attention to violations committed on specific groups, including <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a>, racial/ethnic/religious minorities, women and those executed or on <a href="/wiki/Death_Row" class="mw-redirect" title="Death Row">Death Row</a>. The death penalty report <i>When the State Kills</i><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and the "Human Rights are Women's Rights" campaign were key actions for the latter two issues.
</p><p>During the 1990s, Amnesty International was forced to react to human rights violations occurring in the context of a proliferation of armed conflict in <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Timor" title="East Timor">East Timor</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, and the former <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>. Amnesty International took no position on whether to support or oppose external military interventions in these armed conflicts. It did not reject the use of force, even lethal force, or ask those engaged to lay down their arms. Instead, it questioned the motives behind external intervention and selectivity of international action in relation to the strategic interests of those who sent troops. It argued that action should be taken to prevent human-rights problems from becoming human-rights catastrophes, and that both intervention and inaction represented a failure of the <a href="/wiki/International_community" title="International community">international community</a>.
</p><p>In 1990, when the United States government was deciding whether or not to invade <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, a Kuwaiti woman, known to Congress by her first name only, Nayirah, told the congress that when Iraq invaded <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, she stayed behind after some of her family left the country. She said she was volunteering in a local hospital when Iraqi soldiers stole the <a href="/wiki/Incubator_(neonatal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Incubator (neonatal)">incubators</a> with children in them and left them to freeze to death. Amnesty International, who had human rights investigators in Kuwait, confirmed the story and helped spread it. The organization also inflated the number of children who were killed by the robbery to over 300, more than the number of incubators available in the city hospitals of the country. It was often cited by people, including the <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Congress" title="Member of Congress">members of Congress</a> who voted to approve the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, as one of the reasons to fight. After the war, it was found that the woman was lying, the story was made up, and her last name was not given, because her father was a delegate for Kuwait's government at the same congressional hearing.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, when AI wanted to promote how <a href="/wiki/Shell_Oil_Company" title="Shell Oil Company">Shell Oil Company</a> was involved with the execution of an environmental and human-rights activist <a href="/wiki/Ken_Saro-Wiwa" title="Ken Saro-Wiwa">Ken Saro-Wiwa</a> in Nigeria, it was stopped. Newspapers and advertising companies refused to run AI's ads because Shell Oil was a customer of theirs as well. Shell's main argument was that it was drilling oil in a country that already violated human rights and had no way to enforce human-rights policies. To combat the buzz that AI was trying to create, it immediately publicized how Shell was helping to improve overall life in Nigeria. <a href="/wiki/Salil_Shetty" title="Salil Shetty">Salil Shetty</a>, the director of Amnesty, said, "Social media re-energises the idea of the global citizen".<sup id="cite_ref-guardian1_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> James M. Russell notes how the drive for profit from private media sources conflicts with the stories that AI wants to be heard.<sup id="cite_ref-Russell_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amnesty International was proactive in pushing for recognition of the universality of human rights. The campaign 'Get Up, Sign Up' marked 50 years of the UDHR. Thirteen million pledges were collected in support, and the Decl music concert was held in Paris on 10 December 1998 (<a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Day" title="Human Rights Day">Human Rights Day</a>). At the intergovernmental level, Amnesty International argued in favour of creating a <a href="/wiki/Office_of_the_United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Human_Rights" title="Office of the United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights">United Nations High Commissioner for Human Rights</a> (established 1993) and an <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> (established 2002).
</p><p>After his arrest in London in 1998 by the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Metropolitan Police">Metropolitan Police</a>, Amnesty International became involved in the legal battle of Senator <a href="/wiki/Augusto_Pinochet" title="Augusto Pinochet">Augusto Pinochet</a>, former Chilean dictator, who sought to avoid extradition to Spain to face charges. <a href="/wiki/Lord_Hoffman" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Hoffman">Lord Hoffman</a> had an indirect connection with Amnesty International, and this led to an important test for the appearance of bias in legal proceedings in UK law. There was a suit<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> against the decision to release Senator Pinochet, taken by the then British <a href="/wiki/Home_Secretary" title="Home Secretary">Home Secretary</a> Jack Straw, before that decision had actually been taken, in an attempt to prevent the release of Senator Pinochet. The English <a href="/wiki/High_Court_of_Justice" title="High Court of Justice">High Court</a> refused<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> the application, and Senator Pinochet was released and returned to Chile.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000s">2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After 2000, Amnesty International's primary focus turned to the challenges arising from <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a> and the reaction to the <a href="/wiki/11_September_2001_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="11 September 2001 attacks">11 September 2001 attacks</a> in the United States. The issue of globalization provoked a major shift in Amnesty International policy, as the scope of its work was widened to include economic, social and cultural rights, an area that it had declined to work on in the past. Amnesty International felt this shift was important, not just to give credence to its principle of the indivisibility of rights, but because of what it saw as the growing power of companies and the undermining of many nation states as a result of globalization.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the aftermath of 11 September attacks, the new Amnesty International Secretary General, <a href="/wiki/Irene_Khan" title="Irene Khan">Irene Khan</a>, reported that a senior government official had said to Amnesty International delegates: "Your role collapsed with the collapse of the Twin Towers in New York."<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In the years following the attacks, some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2010)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> believe that the gains made by human rights organizations over previous decades had possibly been eroded.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Amnesty International argued that human rights were the basis for the security of all, not a barrier to it. Criticism came directly from the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_W._Bush" title="Presidency of George W. Bush">Bush administration</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, when Khan, in 2005, likened the US government's detention facility at <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_detention_camp" title="Guantanamo Bay detention camp">Guantanamo Bay, Cuba</a>, to a <a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">Soviet Gulag</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the first half of the new decade, Amnesty International turned its attention to <a href="/wiki/Violence_against_women" title="Violence against women">violence against women</a>, controls on the world <a href="/wiki/Arms_industry" title="Arms industry">arms trade</a>, concerns surrounding the effectiveness of the UN, and ending torture.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> With its membership close to two million by 2005,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Amnesty continued to work for prisoners of conscience.
</p><p>In 2007, AI's executive committee decided to support access to abortion "within reasonable gestational limits...for women in cases of rape, incest or violence, or where the pregnancy jeopardizes a mother's life or health".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amnesty International reported, concerning the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>, on 17 March 2008, that despite claims the security situation in Iraq has improved in recent months, the human rights situation is disastrous, after the start of the war five years earlier in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, Amnesty International accused Israel and the Palestinian Hamas movement of committing war crimes during Israel's January offensive in Gaza, called <a href="/wiki/Operation_Cast_Lead" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Cast Lead">Operation Cast Lead</a>, that resulted in the deaths of more than 1,400 Palestinians and 13 Israelis.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The 117-page Amnesty report charged Israeli forces with killing hundreds of civilians and wanton destruction of thousands of homes. Amnesty found evidence of Israeli soldiers using Palestinian civilians as human shields. A subsequent <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Fact_Finding_Mission_on_the_Gaza_Conflict" title="United Nations Fact Finding Mission on the Gaza Conflict">United Nations Fact Finding Mission on the Gaza Conflict</a> was carried out; Amnesty stated that its findings were consistent with those of Amnesty's own field investigation, and called on the UN to act promptly to implement the mission's recommendations.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s">2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg/220px-Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg/330px-Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg/440px-Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3847" data-file-height="2885" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aktionstag_anl%C3%A4sslich_des_100._Internationalen_Frauentages_-_Amnesty_International.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amnesty International, 19 March 2011.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg/220px-Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg/330px-Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg/440px-Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg 2x" data-file-width="632" data-file-height="548" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meguro_2014_amnesty_international.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Japanese branch of Amnesty International, 23 May 2014.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2010">2010</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 2010">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In February 2010, <a href="/wiki/Gita_Sahgal#Amnesty_International_controversy" title="Gita Sahgal">Amnesty suspended</a> <a href="/wiki/Gita_Sahgal" title="Gita Sahgal">Gita Sahgal</a>, its gender unit head, after she criticized Amnesty for its links with <a href="/wiki/Moazzam_Begg" title="Moazzam Begg">Moazzam Begg</a>, director of <a href="/wiki/Cageprisoners" class="mw-redirect" title="Cageprisoners">Cageprisoners</a>. She said it was "a gross error of judgment" to work with "Britain's most famous supporter of the Taliban".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JoanSmith_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JoanSmith-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Amnesty responded that Sahgal was not suspended "for raising these issues internally... [Begg] speaks about his own views ..., not Amnesty International's".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Among those who spoke up for Sahgal were <a href="/wiki/Salman_Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie">Salman Rushdie</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Member of Parliament <a href="/wiki/Denis_MacShane" title="Denis MacShane">Denis MacShane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Smith_(writer)" class="mw-redirect" title="Joan Smith (writer)">Joan Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hitchens" title="Christopher Hitchens">Christopher Hitchens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bright" title="Martin Bright">Martin Bright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melanie_Phillips" title="Melanie Phillips">Melanie Phillips</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nick_Cohen" title="Nick Cohen">Nick Cohen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JoanSmith_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JoanSmith-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-denis_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-denis-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mel_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mel-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2011">2011</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 2011">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In February 2011, Amnesty requested that Swiss authorities start a criminal investigation of former US President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> and arrest him.<sup id="cite_ref-AIBush_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIBush-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2011, Amnesty International celebrated its 50 years with an animated short film directed by <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Lascano" title="Carlos Lascano">Carlos Lascano</a>, produced by Eallin Motion Art and Dreamlife Studio, with music by Academy Award-winner <a href="/wiki/Hans_Zimmer" title="Hans Zimmer">Hans Zimmer</a> and nominee Lorne Balfe. The film shows that the fight for humanity is not yet over.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2012">2012</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 2012">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In August 2012, Amnesty International's chief executive in India sought an impartial investigation, led by the United Nations, to render justice to those affected by war crimes in Sri Lanka.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2014">2014</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2014">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg/220px-Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg/330px-Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg/440px-Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3211" data-file-height="2141" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cologne_Germany_Cologne-Gay-Pride-2014_Parade-12.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Supporters of Amnesty International at Cologne Pride Parade 2014</div></div></div>
<p>On 18 August 2014, in the wake of <a href="/wiki/Ferguson_unrest" title="Ferguson unrest">demonstrations</a> sparked by people protesting the <a href="/wiki/Shooting_of_Michael_Brown" title="Shooting of Michael Brown">fatal police shooting of Michael Brown</a>, an unarmed 18-year-old man, and subsequent acquittal of Darren Wilson, the officer who shot him, Amnesty International sent a 13-person contingent of human rights activists to seek meetings with officials as well as to train local activists in non-violent protest methods.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters.Amnesty_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters.Amnesty-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> This was the first time that the organization has deployed such a team to the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In a press release, AI USA director <a href="/wiki/Steven_W._Hawkins" title="Steven W. Hawkins">Steven W. Hawkins</a> said, "The U.S. cannot continue to allow those obligated and duty-bound to protect to become those who their community fears most."<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2016">2016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 2016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In February 2016, Amnesty International launched its annual report of human rights around the world titled "The State of the World's Human Rights". It warns from the consequences of "us vs them" speech which divided human beings into two camps. It states that this speech enhances a global pushback against human rights and makes the world more divided and more dangerous. It also states that in 2016, governments turned a blind eye to war crimes and passed laws that violate <a href="/wiki/Free_Expression_Policy_Project" title="Free Expression Policy Project">free expression</a>. Donald Trump signed an executive order in an attempt to prevent refugees from seeking resettlement in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Elsewhere, China, Egypt, Ethiopia, India, Iran, Thailand and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> carried out massive crackdowns, while authorities in other countries continued to implement security measures represent an infringement on rights.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In June 2016, Amnesty International has called on the United Nations General Assembly to "immediately suspend" <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> from the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Council" title="United Nations Human Rights Council">UN Human Rights Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Richard Bennett, head of Amnesty's UN Office, said: "The credibility of the U.N. Human Rights Council is at stake. Since joining the council, Saudi Arabia's dire human rights record at home has continued to deteriorate and the coalition it leads has unlawfully killed and injured thousands of civilians in the <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen" title="Saudi Arabian-led intervention in Yemen">conflict in Yemen</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2016, Amnesty International revealed that <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_Victims" title="Voiceless Victims">Voiceless Victims</a>, a fake non-profit organization which claims to raise awareness for migrant workers who are victims of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Qatar" title="Human rights in Qatar">human rights abuses</a> in <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a>, had been trying to spy on their staff.<sup id="cite_ref-lemondecommentuneongfantome_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lemondecommentuneongfantome-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-forbesthisfakenonprofit_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbesthisfakenonprofit-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2017">2017</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: 2017">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg/220px-WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg/330px-WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg/440px-WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5355" data-file-height="3012" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WorldPride_2017_-_Madrid_-_Manifestaci%C3%B3n_-_170701_210636.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amnesty International sign at the <a href="/wiki/Madrid_Pride" title="Madrid Pride">WorldPride Madrid</a> in July 2017</div></div></div>
<p>Amnesty International published its annual report for the year 2016–2017 on 21 February 2017. Secretary General <a href="/wiki/Salil_Shetty" title="Salil Shetty">Salil Shetty</a>'s opening statement in the report highlighted many ongoing international abuses as well as emerging threats. Shetty drew attention, among many issues, to the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>, the use of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemical weapons">chemical weapons</a> in the <a href="/wiki/War_in_Darfur" title="War in Darfur">War in Darfur</a>, outgoing United States President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>'s expansion of <a href="/wiki/Drone_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Drone warfare">drone warfare</a>, and the successful 2016 presidential election campaign of Obama's successor <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>. Shetty stated that the Trump election campaign was characterized by "poisonous" discourse in which "he frequently made deeply divisive statements marked by misogyny and xenophobia, and pledged to roll back established civil liberties and introduce policies which would be profoundly inimical to human rights." In his opening summary, Shetty stated that "the world in 2016 became a darker and more unstable place."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2017, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkish</a> police detained 10 human rights activists during a workshop on digital security at a hotel near <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. Eight people, including <a href="/wiki/Idil_Eser" class="mw-redirect" title="Idil Eser">Idil Eser</a>, Amnesty International director in Turkey, as well as German <a href="/wiki/Peter_Steudtner" title="Peter Steudtner">Peter Steudtner</a> and Swede Ali Gharavi, were arrested. Two others were detained but released pending trial. They were accused of aiding armed terror organizations in alleged communications with suspects linked to <a href="/wiki/Kurd" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurd">Kurdish</a> and left-wing militants, as well as the movement led by US-based Muslim cleric <a href="/wiki/Fethullah_Gulen" class="mw-redirect" title="Fethullah Gulen">Fethullah Gulen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Amnesty International supported the UN treaty on the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Prohibition_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons">Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons</a>. James Lynch, Head of Arms Control and Human Rights at Amnesty International, said: "This historic treaty brings us a step closer to a world free from the horrors of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, the most destructive and indiscriminate weapons ever created."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2018">2018</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: 2018">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg/220px-Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg/330px-Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg/440px-Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2658" data-file-height="1772" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amnesty_Wiki-a-thon_May_19th_Amsterdam_-_group_photo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A protest calling for the release of detained <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi</a> <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights_in_Saudi_Arabia" title="Women&#39;s rights in Saudi Arabia">women's rights</a> activists in May 2018</div></div></div>
<p>Amnesty International published its 2017/2018 report in February 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2018, an Amnesty International researcher was abducted and beaten while observing demonstrations in Magas, the capital of Ingushetia, Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 25 October, federal officers raided the <a href="/wiki/Bengaluru" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengaluru">Bengaluru</a> office for 10 hours on a suspicion that the organization had violated foreign direct investment guidelines on the orders of the <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_Directorate" title="Enforcement Directorate">Enforcement Directorate</a>.  Employees and supporters of Amnesty International say this is an act to <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_fear" title="Culture of fear">intimidate organizations</a> and people who question the authority and capabilities of government leaders. Aakar Patel, the Executive Director of the Indian branch claimed, "The Enforcement Directorate's raid on our office today shows how the authorities are now treating <a href="/wiki/Human_rights_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="Human rights organizations">human rights organizations</a> like criminal enterprises, using heavy-handed methods. The current Prime Minister of India, <a href="/wiki/Narendra_Modi" title="Narendra Modi">Narendra Modi</a>, has been criticized for harming civil society in India, specifically by targeting advocacy groups.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Modi has cancelled the registration of about 15,000 nongovernmental organizations under the <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Contribution_Regulation_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Foreign Contribution Regulation Act">Foreign Contribution Regulation Act</a> (FCRA); the U.N. has issued statemenets against the policies that allow these cancellations to occur.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Though nothing was found to confirm these accusations, the government plans on continuing the investigation and has frozen the <a href="/wiki/Bank_account" title="Bank account">bank accounts</a> of all the offices in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. A spokesperson for the Enforcement Directorate has said the investigation could take three months to complete.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 30 October 2018, Amnesty called for the arrest and prosecution of <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigerian</a> security forces claiming that they used excessive force against Shi’a protesters during a peaceful religious procession around Abuja, Nigeria. At least 45 were killed and 122 were injured during the event .<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2018, Amnesty reported the arrest of 19 or more rights activists and lawyers in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. The arrests were made by the Egyptian authorities as part of the regime's ongoing crackdown on dissent. One of the arrested was Hoda Abdel-Monaim, a 60-year-old human rights lawyer and former member of the National Council for Human Rights. Amnesty reported that following the arrests Egyptian Coordination for Rights and Freedoms (ECRF) decided to suspend its activities due to the hostile environment towards civil society in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 5 December 2018, Amnesty International strongly condemned the execution of <a href="/wiki/Ihar_Hershankou" title="Ihar Hershankou">Ihar Hershankou</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Siamion_Berazhnoy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Siamion Berazhnoy (page does not exist)">Siamion Berazhnoy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> They were shot despite <a href="/wiki/UN_Human_Rights_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Human Rights Committee">UN Human Rights Committee</a> request for a delay.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2019">2019</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: 2019">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg/220px-Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg/330px-Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg/440px-Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marche_des_fiert%C3%A9s_rouen_20190504_-_amnesty_international.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amnesty International sign in <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>, 4 May 2019</div></div></div>
<p>In February 2019, Amnesty International's management team offered to resign after an <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Amnesty_International#2019_Bullying_Report" title="Criticism of Amnesty International">independent report</a> found what it called a "toxic culture" of <a href="/wiki/Workplace_bullying" title="Workplace bullying">workplace bullying</a>, and found evidence of <a href="/wiki/Bullying" title="Bullying">bullying</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harassment" title="Harassment">harassment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexism" title="Sexism">sexism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, after being asked to investigate the <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">suicides</a> of 30-year Amnesty veteran Gaetan Mootoo in Paris in May 2018 (who left a <a href="/wiki/Suicide_note" title="Suicide note">note</a> citing work pressures), and 28-year-old intern Rosalind McGregor in Geneva in July 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-RTE2019-02-23a_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RTE2019-02-23a-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2019, Amnesty International's deputy director for research in Europe, Massimo Moratti, warned that if extradited to the United States, <a href="/wiki/WikiLeaks" title="WikiLeaks">WikiLeaks</a> founder <a href="/wiki/Julian_Assange" title="Julian Assange">Julian Assange</a> would face the "risk of serious human rights violations, namely detention conditions, which could violate the prohibition of torture".<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 24 April 2019 an alliance of different groups of <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> activists occupied the reception of Amnesty's London offices, to protest against Amnesty's inaction in ignoring the human rights abuses against <a href="/wiki/Kurds_in_Turkey" title="Kurds in Turkey">Kurds in Turkey</a>, including the incarceration and isolation of their leader, Abdullah Øcalan.  There were claims that Amnesty's inaction had been driven by undue deference to the <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatari</a> regimes. A hunger strike was declared by the occupiers.  On 26 April Amnesty called on the Police forcibly to eject the demonstrators, and the offices were cleared.  There was subsequent public criticism of this action.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On May 14, 2019, Amnesty International filed a petition with the District Court of Tel Aviv, Israel, seeking a revocation of the export license of surveillance technology firm <a href="/wiki/NSO_Group" title="NSO Group">NSO Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> The filing states that "staff of Amnesty International have an ongoing and well-founded fear they may continue to be targeted and ultimately surveilled"<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> by NSO technology. Other lawsuits have also been filed against NSO in Israeli courts over alleged human-rights abuses, including a December 2018 filing by Saudi dissident Omar Abdulaziz, who claimed NSO's software targeted his phone during a period in which he was in regular contact with murdered journalist <a href="/wiki/Jamal_Khashoggi" title="Jamal Khashoggi">Jamal Kashoggi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Buchanan_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buchanan-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In August 2019, the Global Assembly elected five new Members to the International Board - Tiumalu Peter Fa'afiu (New Zealand), Dr Anjhula Singh Bais (Malaysia), Ritz Lee Santos III (The Philippines), Lulu Barera (Mexico) and Aniket Shah (USA) as Treasurer. Given Fa'afiu received the most votes, his term will be for four years and others three years. Bais and Santos become the first Malaysian and Filipino elected. Fa'afiu the first of Pacific descent. They join at a significant time in the organisation's history - financial challenges, organisational restructure, development of a new global strategy, ever shrinking civil society space, and a demand from its younger members and partners to move into non-traditional areas such as climate change.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In September 2019, European Commission President-elect <a href="/wiki/Ursula_von_der_Leyen" title="Ursula von der Leyen">Ursula von der Leyen</a> created the new position of "Vice President for <a href="/wiki/Von_der_Leyen_Commission" title="Von der Leyen Commission">Protecting our European Way of Life</a>", who will be responsible for upholding the rule-of-law, internal security and migration.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> Amnesty International accused the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> of "using the framing of the far right" by linking migration with security.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At its Board Meeting in October 2019, International Board members appointed Sarah Beamish (Canada) as Chairperson. She has been on the Board since 2015 and at age 34 is the youngest IB Chair in its history. She is a human rights lawyer in her homeland.
</p><p>On November 24, 2019 <a href="/wiki/Anil_Raj" title="Anil Raj">Anil Raj</a> a former board member of Amnesty International board member, was killed by a car bomb while working with the United Nations Development Project. U.S. Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pompeo" title="Mike Pompeo">Mike Pompeo</a> announced Raj’s death at a briefing Nov. 26, during which he discussed other acts of terrorism.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On December 5, 2019 Kumi Naidoo, the organization’s Secretary General, has made the decision to step down from his position due to health-related reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amnesty_International&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg/400px-Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg/600px-Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg/800px-Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="477" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amnesty_International_Sections_2012.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amnesty International Sections, 2012</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Goodwin_House2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Goodwin_House2.JPG/220px-Goodwin_House2.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Goodwin_House2.JPG/330px-Goodwin_House2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Goodwin_House2.JPG/440px-Goodwin_House2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Goodwin_House2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Amnesty Canadian headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Amnesty International is largely made up of voluntary members, but retains a small number of paid professionals. In countries in which Amnesty International has a strong presence, members are organized as "sections". Sections co-ordinate basic Amnesty International activities normally with a significant number of members, some of whom will form into "groups", and a professional staff. Each have a board of directors. 
In 2019 there were 63 sections worldwide. "Structures" are aspiring sections. They also co-ordinate basic activities but have a smaller membership and a limited staff. In countries where no section or structure exists, people can become "international members". Two other organizational models exist: "international networks", which promote specific themes or have a specific identity, and "affiliated groups", which do the same work as section groups, but in isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The highest governing body is the Global Assembly which meets annually. Each Section sends its Chair and Executive Director to the GA. The GA process is governed and managed by the PrepCom (Preparatory Committee).
</p><p>The International Board (formerly known as the International Executive Committee [IEC]), led by the International Board Chairperson (Sarah Beamish) consists of nine members and the International Treasurer. Two members are co-opted.
</p><p>The IB is elected by, and accountable to, the Global Assembly. The International Board meets at least two times during any one year and in practice meets face to face at least four times a year. Other board and sub committee meetings are undertaken via video conferencing.
</p><p>The role of the International Board is to take decisions on behalf of Amnesty International, govern the International Secretariat including regional offices, implement the strategy laid out by the Global Assembly and ensure compliance with the organization's statutes.
</p><p>The International Secretariat (IS) is responsible for the conduct and daily affairs of Amnesty International under direction from the International Board.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> It is run by approximately 500 professional staff members and is headed by a Secretary General. The Secretariat operates several work programmes; International Law and Organizations; Research; Campaigns; Mobilization; and Communications. Its offices have been located in London since its establishment in the mid-1960s.
</p>

<td><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amnistia.org.uy">"amnistia.org.uy"</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=amnistia.org.uy&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.amnistia.org.uy&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAmnesty+International" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International_USA" title="Amnesty International USA">Amnesty International USA</a></td>
<td><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.amnestyusa.org">"amnestyusa.org"</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=amnestyusa.org&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.amnestyusa.org&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAmnesty+International" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r935243608"/>
</td></tr>

<td>Amnesty International Venezuela</td>
<td><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150601160315/http://www.amnistia.me/">"amnistia.me"</a>.
